<meta name="DC.Title" content="Genomic evidence for convergent evolution of gene clusters for momilactone biosynthesis in land plants" />

<meta name="DC.Description" content="The biosynthetic genes of some specialized plant metabolites appear to be clustered in the genomes of higher plants. Momilactones are defense compounds produced in rice and barnyard grass by family-conserved biosynthetic gene clusters (BGCs). We sequenced the genome of Calohypnum plumiforme , a momilactone-producing nonvascular bryophyte, and showed that it also contains a functionally similar momilactone BGC distinguished by its lack of synteny with the clusters found in vascular plants. The expression of the Calohypnum biosynthetic genes in tobacco demonstrated their role in momilactone A production. This is the first report of a BGC for a specialized metabolite in bryophytes. Our findings indicate that the momilactone clusters present in three different plant species may have evolved independently via convergent evolution." />

<meta name="article:section" content="Biological Sciences" />
<meta name="citation_title" content="Genomic evidence for convergent evolution of gene clusters for momilactone biosynthesis in land plants" />
<meta name="citation_abstract" lang="en" content="&lt;p&gt;Momilactones are bioactive diterpenoids that contribute to plant defense against pathogens and allelopathic interactions between plants. Both cultivated and wild grass species of &lt;i&gt;Oryza&lt;/i&gt; and &lt;i&gt;Echinochloa crus-galli&lt;/i&gt; (barnyard grass) produce momilactones using a biosynthetic gene cluster (BGC) in their genomes. The bryophyte &lt;i&gt;Calohypnum plumiforme&lt;/i&gt; (formerly &lt;i&gt;Hypnum plumaeforme&lt;/i&gt;) also produces momilactones, and the bifunctional diterpene cyclase gene &lt;i&gt;CpDTC1/HpDTC1,&lt;/i&gt; which is responsible for the production of the diterpene framework, has been characterized. To understand the molecular architecture of the momilactone biosynthetic genes in the moss genome and their evolutionary relationships with other momilactone-producing plants, we sequenced and annotated the &lt;i&gt;C. plumiforme&lt;/i&gt; genome. The data revealed a 150-kb genomic region that contains two cytochrome P450 genes, the &lt;i&gt;CpDTC1&lt;/i&gt;/&lt;i&gt;HpDTC1&lt;/i&gt; gene and the “dehydrogenase momilactone A synthase” gene tandemly arranged and inductively transcribed following stress exposure. The predicted enzymatic functions in yeast and recombinant assay and the successful pathway reconstitution in &lt;i&gt;Nicotiana benthamiana&lt;/i&gt; suggest that it is a functional BGC responsible for momilactone production. Furthermore, in a survey of genomic sequences of a broad range of plant species, we found that momilactone BGC is limited to the two grasses (&lt;i&gt;Oryza&lt;/i&gt; and &lt;i&gt;Echinochloa&lt;/i&gt;) and &lt;i&gt;C. plumiforme&lt;/i&gt;, with no synteny among these genomes. These results indicate that while the gene cluster in &lt;i&gt;C. plumiforme&lt;/i&gt; is functionally similar to that in rice and barnyard grass, it is likely a product of convergent evolution. To the best of our knowledge, this report of a BGC for a specialized plant defense metabolite in bryophytes is unique.&lt;/p&gt;" />
<meta name="citation_abstract" lang="en" scheme="executive-summary" content="&lt;h3&gt;Significance&lt;/h3&gt;
&lt;p&gt;The biosynthetic genes of some specialized plant metabolites appear to be clustered in the genomes of higher plants. Momilactones are defense compounds produced in rice and barnyard grass by family-conserved biosynthetic gene clusters (BGCs). We sequenced the genome of &lt;i&gt;Calohypnum plumiforme&lt;/i&gt;, a momilactone-producing nonvascular bryophyte, and showed that it also contains a functionally similar momilactone BGC distinguished by its lack of synteny with the clusters found in vascular plants. The expression of the &lt;i&gt;Calohypnum&lt;/i&gt; biosynthetic genes in tobacco demonstrated their role in momilactone A production. This is the first report of a BGC for a specialized metabolite in bryophytes. Our findings indicate that the momilactone clusters present in three different plant species may have evolved independently via convergent evolution.&lt;/p&gt;" />

<meta name="twitter:title" content="Genomic evidence for convergent evolution of gene clusters for momilactone biosynthesis in land plants" />
<meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image" />
<meta name="twitter:image" content="https://www.pnas.org/sites/default/files/highwire/pnas/117/22.cover-source.jpg" />
<meta name="twitter:description" content="The biosynthetic genes of some specialized plant metabolites appear to be clustered in the genomes of higher plants. Momilactones are defense compounds produced in rice and barnyard grass by family-conserved biosynthetic gene clusters (BGCs). We sequenced the genome of Calohypnum plumiforme , a momilactone-producing nonvascular bryophyte, and showed that it also contains a functionally similar momilactone BGC distinguished by its lack of synteny with the clusters found in vascular plants. The expression of the Calohypnum biosynthetic genes in tobacco demonstrated their role in momilactone A production. This is the first report of a BGC for a specialized metabolite in bryophytes. Our findings indicate that the momilactone clusters present in three different plant species may have evolved independently via convergent evolution." />

Momilactones are defense compounds produced in rice and barnyard grass by family-conserved biosynthetic gene clusters (BGCs). We sequenced the genome of Calohypnum plumiforme , a momilactone-producing nonvascular bryophyte, and showed that it also contains a functionally similar momilactone BGC distinguished by its lack of synteny with the clusters found in vascular plants. The expression of the Calohypnum biosynthetic genes in tobacco demonstrated their role in momilactone A production. This is the first report of a BGC for a specialized metabolite in bryophytes. Our findings indicate that the momilactone clusters present in three different plant species may have evolved independently via convergent evolution." />

  <div class="pane-content">
    <div class="highwire-article-citation highwire-citation-type-highwire-article" data-node-nid="929094" id="node929094" data-pisa="pnas;117/22/12472" data-pisa-master="pnas;1914373117" data-apath="/pnas/117/22/12472.atom" data-hw-author-tooltip-instance="highwire_author_tooltip"><div class="highwire-cite highwire-cite-highwire-article highwire-citation-pnas-article-title-complete clearfix has-author-tooltip highwire-citation-highwire-article-top-a">
  
  
      <div class="highwire-cite-overline"><span  class="highwire-cite-metadata-article-category highwire-cite-metadata">Research Article</span></div>

      <a href="/content/117/25/13867" class="highwire-cite-linked-title" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="highwire-cite-title">Why are momilactones always associated with biosynthetic gene clusters in plants?</span></a>  
    	<div class="highwire-cite-metadata">- <span  class="highwire-cite-metadata-date highwire-cite-metadata">Jun 02, 2020 </span></div>
  
</div>

</div></li></ul></div></div></div>  </div>

<div class="panel-separator"></div><div class="panel-pane pane-highwire-panel-tabs-container" >
  
      
  
  <div class="pane-content">
    <div data-panels-ajax-tab-preloaded="jnl_pnas_tab_art" id="panels-ajax-tab-container-highwire_article_tabs" class="panels-ajax-tab-container"><div class="panels-ajax-tab-loading" style ="display:none"><img class="loading" src="https://www.pnas.org/sites/all/modules/contrib/panels_ajax_tab/images/loading.gif" alt="Loading" title="Loading" /></div><div class="panels-ajax-tab-wrap-jnl_pnas_tab_art"><div class="panel-display panel-1col clearfix" >
  <div class="panel-panel panel-col">
    <div><div class="panel-pane pane-highwire-markup" >
  
      
  
  <div class="pane-content">
    <div class="highwire-markup"><div xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="content-block-markup" data-highwire-cite-ref-tooltip-instance="highwire_reflinks_tooltip" xmlns:xhtml="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml"><div class="article fulltext-view "><span class="highwire-journal-article-marker-start"></span><div class="executive-summary"><h2 class="">Significance</h2><p id="p-5">The biosynthetic genes of some specialized plant metabolites appear to be clustered in the genomes of higher plants. Momilactones are defense compounds produced in rice and barnyard grass by family-conserved biosynthetic gene clusters (BGCs). We sequenced the genome of <em>Calohypnum plumiforme</em>, a momilactone-producing nonvascular bryophyte, and showed that it also contains a functionally similar momilactone BGC distinguished by its lack of synteny with the clusters found in vascular plants. The expression of the <em>Calohypnum</em> biosynthetic genes in tobacco demonstrated their role in momilactone A production. This is the first report of a BGC for a specialized metabolite in bryophytes. Our findings indicate that the momilactone clusters present in three different plant species may have evolved independently via convergent evolution.</p></div><div class="section abstract" id="abstract-2"><h2>Abstract</h2><p id="p-6">Momilactones are bioactive diterpenoids that contribute to plant defense against pathogens and allelopathic interactions between plants. Both cultivated and wild grass species of <em>Oryza</em> and <em>Echinochloa crus-galli</em> (barnyard grass) produce momilactones using a biosynthetic gene cluster (BGC) in their genomes. The bryophyte <em>Calohypnum plumiforme</em> (formerly <em>Hypnum plumaeforme</em>) also produces momilactones, and the bifunctional diterpene cyclase gene <em>CpDTC1/HpDTC1,</em> which is responsible for the production of the diterpene framework, has been characterized. To understand the molecular architecture of the momilactone biosynthetic genes in the moss genome and their evolutionary relationships with other momilactone-producing plants, we sequenced and annotated the <em>C. plumiforme</em> genome. The data revealed a 150-kb genomic region that contains two cytochrome P450 genes, the <em>CpDTC1</em>/<em>HpDTC1</em> gene and the “dehydrogenase momilactone A synthase” gene tandemly arranged and inductively transcribed following stress exposure. The predicted enzymatic functions in yeast and recombinant assay and the successful pathway reconstitution in <em>Nicotiana benthamiana</em> suggest that it is a functional BGC responsible for momilactone production. Furthermore, in a survey of genomic sequences of a broad range of plant species, we found that momilactone BGC is limited to the two grasses (<em>Oryza</em> and <em>Echinochloa</em>) and <em>C. plumiforme</em>, with no synteny among these genomes. These results indicate that while the gene cluster in <em>C. plumiforme</em> is functionally similar to that in rice and barnyard grass, it is likely a product of convergent evolution. To the best of our knowledge, this report of a BGC for a specialized plant defense metabolite in bryophytes is unique.</p></div><ul class="kwd-group"><li class="kwd"><a href="/keyword/specialized-metabolites" class="hw-term hw-article-keyword hw-article-keyword-specialized-metabolites" rel="nofollow">specialized metabolites</a></li><li class="kwd"><a href="/keyword/gene-cluster" class="hw-term hw-article-keyword hw-article-keyword-gene-cluster" rel="nofollow">gene cluster</a></li><li class="kwd"><a href="/keyword/bryophytes" class="hw-term hw-article-keyword hw-article-keyword-bryophytes" rel="nofollow">Bryophytes</a></li><li class="kwd"><a href="/keyword/convergent-evolution" class="hw-term hw-article-keyword hw-article-keyword-convergent-evolution" rel="nofollow">convergent evolution</a></li><li class="kwd"><a href="/keyword/diterpene-momilactones" class="hw-term hw-article-keyword hw-article-keyword-diterpene-momilactones" rel="nofollow">diterpene momilactones</a></li></ul><p id="p-7" class="flushleft">Momilactones, including the two major structurally diverged forms momilactone A and B, originally isolated from the hulls of cultivated rice <em>Oryza sativa</em> (<em>Os</em>), are specialized diterpenoid metabolites, with the potential to competitively inhibit the growth of other plants and microorganisms, and thought to function as a chemical defense against pathogens (<a id="xref-ref-1-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-1">1</a><a id="xref-ref-2-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-2"><span>⇓</span></a><a id="xref-ref-3-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-3"><span>⇓</span></a><a id="xref-ref-4-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-4"><span>⇓</span></a><a id="xref-ref-5-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-5"><span>⇓</span></a>–<a id="xref-ref-6-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-6">6</a>). In addition to their original physiological roles in plants, it is also reported that these molecules have cytotoxic and antitumor activity in cancer cell lines (<a id="xref-ref-7-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-7">7</a>). Therefore, momilactones exhibit a broad range of growth-inhibitory effects in various organisms, such as animals, plants, fungi, and bacteria.</p><p id="p-8">In addition to <em>O. sativa</em>, some wild-rice species in the genus <em>Oryza</em> are also known to produce momilactones (<a id="xref-ref-8-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-8">8</a>). Moreover, we previously reported that the moss <em>Calohypnum plumiforme</em> (<em>Cp</em>) (<em>Hypnum plumaeforme</em> [<em>Hp</em>] prior to 2019 in the National Center for Biotechnology Information) produces momilactone as a chemical defense compound and the <em>CpDTC1</em>/<em>HpDTC1</em> gene, by encoding a stress-inducible bifunctional diterpene cyclase (DTC) (<em>syn</em>-pimaradiene synthase), is key in the first committed step of the momilactone biosynthetic pathway (<a id="xref-ref-9-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-9">9</a>). Feeding experiments using uniformly labeled <sup>13</sup>C-<em>syn</em>-pimaradiene have demonstrated the existence of an <em>in planta</em> biosynthetic route of momilactone production from <em>syn</em>-pimaradiene in <em>C. plumiforme</em> (<a id="xref-ref-10-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-10">10</a>). Thus, momilactones are specialized diterpenoid metabolites produced in evolutionally highly diverged plant species.</p><p id="p-9">The biosynthetic genes of a number of specialized metabolites produced in plants have been shown to be clustered in the genome of the plants in which they are found. For example, the biosynthetic gene cluster (BGC) for DIMBOA (2,4-dihydroxy-7-methoxy-1,4-benzoxazin-3-one, a naturally occurring hydroxamic acid) located on maize chromosome 4, was the first reported example of operonic gene clustering in higher plants associated with allelochemical production (<a id="xref-ref-11-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-11">11</a>). Oat avenacin, a specialized triterpenoid saponin, is also the product of clustered genes in the oat genome (<a id="xref-ref-12-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-12">12</a>). More recent examples of plant BGCs are those forming diterpenoid phytoalexins, including momilactones in rice. Initially it was noted that the terpene cyclase genes for diterpenoid phytoalexins are located within a narrow genomic region of the rice chromosome (<a id="xref-ref-13-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-13">13</a>). Subsequently it was found that genes encoding enzymes functioning at later steps of the momilactones biosynthetic pathway are within this region and their enzymatic functions were confirmed. In fact the majority of the momilactone A biosynthetic genes, including <em>OsCPS4</em> (<em>syn</em>-CDP synthase), <em>OsKSL4</em> (<em>syn</em>-pimaradiene synthase), <em>CYP99A2</em>, <em>CYP99A3</em> (Cytochrome P450 monooxygenase [CYP] for <em>syn</em>-pimaradiene oxidations), and <em>OsMAS</em> (dehydrogenase momilactone synthase) are clustered on chromosome 4 in rice and coordinately regulated under stress conditions (<a id="xref-ref-14-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-14">14</a>, <a id="xref-ref-15-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-15">15</a>). While the biosynthetic genes involved momilactone A formation are known, the genes that encode the enzymes mediating the conversion steps of momilactone A to B are still unknown among all momilactone-producing plants.</p><p id="p-10">Recently, <em>Echinochloa crus-galli</em> (<em>Ec</em>, barnyard grass) has been shown to possess one copy of the momilactone gene cluster in its genome. Thus, all of the momilactone-producing higher plants reported thus far appear to have a family-conserved BGC in their genomes. In addition, the expression of the orthologous genes <em>EcKSL</em> and <em>EcMAS</em> in the <em>E. crus-galli</em> momilactone A gene cluster are coordinately induced upon pathogen infection (<a id="xref-ref-16-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-16">16</a>). As mentioned above, the nonvascular moss <em>C. plumiforme</em> also produces momilactones. However, the clustering (or lack thereof) of momilactone biosynthesis genes in the moss genome was not previously examined since the only available moss genome sequence was that of <em>Physcomitrella patens</em>, reported by Rensing et al. (<a id="xref-ref-17-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-17">17</a>) over a decade ago, with no evidence that <em>P. patens</em> produces any momilactones. Moreover, <em>Physcomitrella</em> species are phylogenetically very distant from the <em>Calohypnum</em> mosses (<a id="xref-ref-17-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-17">17</a><a id="xref-ref-18-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-18"><span>⇓</span></a>–<a id="xref-ref-19-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-19">19</a>).</p><p id="p-11">The evolution of gene clusters has been hypothesized to proceed by recruitment of genes from elsewhere in plant genomes via gene duplication and neofunctionalization, rather than by horizontal gene transfer from other organisms, such as fungi and microbes. It has also been suggested that partial clustering of plant genes occurs in different metabolic pathways by the duplication of functionally related gene pairs and modules (<a id="xref-ref-20-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-20">20</a>). At present, there is little evidentially supported information to aid in understanding the evolution of gene clustering among plant species and how this leads to the formation of specialized metabolites. Moreover, there are no studies that describe the existence and nature of biosynthetic gene clustering in bryophytes and other nonseed-forming plants.</p><p id="p-12">In this study, to examine the evolution of BGCs for momilactone formation, we sequenced the <em>C. plumiforme</em> genome and provide evidence of a momilactone BGC in this nonvascular plant. We further show that the genes in this cluster are inducible and their encoded products are responsible for momilactone formation. Phylogenetic analysis of the clustered genes and syntenic comparisons of the gene cluster organization in the genomes of species producing momilactones indicate that these clusters might have evolved independently via convergent evolution in each plant.</p><div class="section results" id="sec-1"><h2 class="">Results</h2><div id="sec-2" class="subsection"><h3>Genome Sequencing, Assembly, and Annotation.</h3><p id="p-13">The genome survey of <em>C. plumiforme</em> suggested that it is a typical simple diploid genome with low heterozygosity and repeat content, with an estimated genome size of ∼434 Mb (<a id="xref-table-wrap-1-1" class="xref-table" href="#T1">Table 1</a> and <a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1914373117/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw" target="_blank"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S1</a>). The <em>C. plumiforme</em> genome sequencing was performed through the combination of Illumina and PacBio Single Molecule Real Time (SMRT) technology (<a id="xref-table-wrap-1-2" class="xref-table" href="#T1">Table 1</a> and <a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1914373117/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw" target="_blank"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S1<em>A</em></a>). For Illumina sequencing, 350-bp to 20-kb insertion libraries were constructed with one PacBio sequencing cell data. In order to identify the candidate momilactone gene cluster, we tested several strategies to assemble the <em>C. plumiforme</em> genome and determined that the best genome assembly v5.0 (V5.0) was achieved based on the data from the hybrid approach with both Illumina and PacBio sequencing reads (<a id="xref-table-wrap-1-3" class="xref-table" href="#T1">Table 1</a> and <a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1914373117/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw" target="_blank"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S2</a>). The final V5.0 assembly has an accumulated length of 335 Mb, accounting for 77% of the estimated genome size (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1914373117/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw" target="_blank"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S3</a>). Benchmarking universal single-copy orthologs (BUSCO) were applied to evaluate the completeness of the <em>C. plumiforme</em> assembly. Based on the Viridiplantae BUSCO datasets (V3) (<a id="xref-ref-21-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-21">21</a>), we found that 93.9% of the gene space was covered. The gene space coverage for the <em>C. plumiforme</em> assembly is similar to that reported for another moss genome, <em>P. patens</em> (94.2%).</p><div id="T1" class="table pos-float"><div class="table-inline table-callout-links"><div class="callout"><span>View this table:</span><ul class="callout-links"><li class="view-inline first"><a href="##" class="table-expand-inline" data-table-url="/highwire/markup/929271/expansion?postprocessors=highwire_tables%2Chighwire_reclass%2Chighwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_inline_linked_media%2Chighwire_embed&amp;table-expand-inline=1" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View inline</a></li>
<li class="view-popup last"><a href="/highwire/markup/929271/expansion?width=1000&amp;height=500&amp;iframe=true&amp;postprocessors=highwire_tables%2Chighwire_reclass%2Chighwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_inline_linked_media%2Chighwire_embed" class="colorbox colorbox-load table-expand-popup" rel="gallery-fragment-tables" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View popup</a></li>
</ul></div></div><div class="table-caption"><span class="table-label">Table 1.</span> <p id="p-14" class="first-child">Summary of the <em>C. plumiforme</em> genome assembly</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-16">To annotate repeat elements in the <em>C. plumiforme</em> genome, we first built the de novo repeat library from the assembled genome. We identified 164.8 Mb of repetitive elements (16.41% long terminal-repeat elements) covering 49.17% of the assembled genome (<a id="xref-table-wrap-1-4" class="xref-table" href="#T1">Table 1</a> and <a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1914373117/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw" target="_blank"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S4</a>). De novo gene-structure predictions were carried out based on combined evidence from ab initio prediction using AUGUSTUS/GeneMark.hmm/FGENESH and similarity searches (for details, see <a id="xref-sec-8-1" class="xref-sec" href="#sec-8"><em>Materials and Methods</em></a> and <a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1914373117/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw" target="_blank"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S2</a>). The annotated coding sequences of moss <em>P. patens</em> (v3.3), liverwort <em>Marchantia polymorpha</em> (v3.1), and RNA-sequencing (RNA-seq) reads obtained from <em>C. plumiforme</em> in our previous study (<a id="xref-ref-9-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-9">9</a>) were used to perform the homology predictions. All candidate gene models from the above evidence were combined using EVidenceModeler (<a id="xref-ref-22-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-22">22</a>), with a higher weight for the expression evidence (RNA-seq). After removing gene structures with low quality or those containing repeat elements, 32,195 protein-coding genes were finally annotated in the genome assembly (<a id="xref-table-wrap-1-5" class="xref-table" href="#T1">Table 1</a>).</p></div><div id="sec-3" class="subsection"><h3>Evolution of the <em>Calohypnum</em> Genome.</h3><p id="p-17">Mosses are important basal members of the plant kingdom that have diversified to inhabit a wide range of environments worldwide. The genome sequence of only one moss, <em>P. patens</em>, had been reported over a decade ago (<a id="xref-ref-17-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-17">17</a>). Based on annotated genes of the <em>Calohypnum</em> genome assembly and the <em>K</em><sub>s</sub> (rate of synonymous substitution) distribution of paralogous gene pairs, at least one whole-genome duplication event could be identified (<a id="xref-fig-1-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1<em>A</em></a>). The event dates to approximately 8 Mya (<em>K</em><sub>s</sub> peak located at 0.125 to 0.175), modeled after the predicted time of divergence from another moss, <em>P. patens.</em></p><div id="F1" class="fig pos-float type-featured  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/117/22/12472/F1.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Evolution of the Calohypnum genome. (A) Graph showing the Ks (rate of synonymous substitution) distribution of paralogous gene pairs of C. plumiforme and orthologous gene pairs between C. plumiforme and P. patens. The Inset in the panel is a photograph of the two moss species under analysis in this study. (B) Phylogenetic trees of showing the relation between of bryophytes and seed plants based on single-copy genes of their chloroplast genomes. The alga Chara (a nonland plant) was used as outgroup. The divergence time periods (Mya) at branches and time scales are shown." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-193330918" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Evolution of the &lt;em&gt;Calohypnum&lt;/em&gt; genome. (&lt;em&gt;A&lt;/em&gt;) Graph showing the &lt;em&gt;K&lt;/em&gt;s (rate of synonymous substitution) distribution of paralogous gene pairs of &lt;em&gt;C. plumiforme&lt;/em&gt; and orthologous gene pairs between &lt;em&gt;C. plumiforme&lt;/em&gt; and &lt;em&gt;P. patens&lt;/em&gt;. The &lt;em&gt;Inset&lt;/em&gt; in the panel is a photograph of the two moss species under analysis in this study. (&lt;em&gt;B&lt;/em&gt;) Phylogenetic trees of showing the relation between of bryophytes and seed plants based on single-copy genes of their chloroplast genomes. The alga &lt;em&gt;Chara&lt;/em&gt; (a nonland plant) was used as outgroup. The divergence time periods (Mya) at branches and time scales are shown.&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Fig. 1." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/117/22/12472/F1.medium.gif" width="319" height="440"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Fig. 1." src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/117/22/12472/F1.medium.gif" width="319" height="440"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/117/22/12472/F1.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Fig. 1." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li>
<li class="new-tab"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/117/22/12472/F1.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li>
<li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/929462" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li>
</ul></div><div class="fig-caption" xmlns:xhtml="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml"><span class="fig-label">Fig. 1.</span> <p id="p-18" class="first-child">Evolution of the <em>Calohypnum</em> genome. (<em>A</em>) Graph showing the <em>K</em>s (rate of synonymous substitution) distribution of paralogous gene pairs of <em>C. plumiforme</em> and orthologous gene pairs between <em>C. plumiforme</em> and <em>P. patens</em>. The <em>Inset</em> in the panel is a photograph of the two moss species under analysis in this study. (<em>B</em>) Phylogenetic trees of showing the relation between of bryophytes and seed plants based on single-copy genes of their chloroplast genomes. The alga <em>Chara</em> (a nonland plant) was used as outgroup. The divergence time periods (Mya) at branches and time scales are shown.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-19">We also assembled the <em>Calohypnum</em> chloroplast genome using our sequencing data, and with these data, we were able to carry out evolutionary analysis with more species at key evolutionary nodes. Using <em>Chara</em> as outgroup, the phylogenetic tree based on single-copy genes in their chloroplast genomes indicated that the two mosses with available genome sequences separated ∼162 to 190 Mya, similar to the time at which divergence occurred between dicot and monocot plants (∼176 to 189 Mya) (<a id="xref-fig-1-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1<em>B</em></a>). Unsurprisingly, no apparent genomic synteny was found between the two mosses <em>P. patens</em> and <em>C. plumiforme</em> examined in this study. This is reasonable, given their ancient divergence time. Therefore, the genome sequence of <em>C. plumiforme</em> reported in this study provides an important reference point for species in <em>Bryidae</em> (13 orders) and a valuable comparison on a new lineage. Recently, the genome sequence of liverwort <em>M. polymorpha</em> has been reported (<a id="xref-ref-23-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-23">23</a>). We also found that the split between the mosses and other early land plants, such as liverworts, is estimated to have occurred over 450 Mya (<a id="xref-fig-1-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1<em>B</em></a>).</p></div><div id="sec-4" class="subsection"><h3>Identification of a Momilactone Gene Cluster in the <em>Calohypnum</em> Genome.</h3><p id="p-20">In order to identify any putative momilactone gene clusters in the <em>Calohypnum</em> genome, we first searched for orthologs of the <em>O. sativa</em> terpene cyclase genes (<em>OsCPS4</em> and <em>OsKSL4</em>), CYP genes (<em>OsCYP99A2/3</em>), and dehydrogenase momilactone A synthase (MAS) (<em>OsMAS</em>) in the <em>Calohypnum</em> genome assembly. A putative momilactone gene cluster, which includes one terpene synthase gene (<em>CpDTC1</em>/<em>HpDTC1</em>), two CYP homologs, and one MAS homolog within a 150-kb region, was eventually identified in one scaffold (Scaffold38) of the V5.0 assembly built from several contigs (<a id="xref-fig-2-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2<em>A</em></a> and <a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1914373117/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw" target="_blank"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S3</a>). The putative cluster was located at a contig (i.e., no gaps in the 150-kb region).</p><div id="F2" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/117/22/12472/F2.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="The putative gene cluster for momilactone biosynthesis in C. plumiforme. (A) The genomic synteny of the BGCs for momilactone formation in plants. (B) Time course gene-expression profiles of the four genes (CpDTC1/HpDTC1, CpMAS, CpCYP970A14, and CpCYP964A1) found in the momilactone BGC after the treatment of CuCl2 or chitosan. CpMAS: Unigene_5051, LC494432; CpCYP970A14: Unigene_16484, LC494433; CpDTC1/HpDTC1: LC128408; CpCYP964A1: Unigene_12783, LC494434. The data shown were normalized by comparison to the expression of the housekeeping gene CpACT3. (C) Comparison of basal expression levels of the four genes in the cluster. The expression data represent the mean &#xB1; SD of three biological replicates with independent elicitor treatments to C. plumiforme gametophytes." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-193330918" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;The putative gene cluster for momilactone biosynthesis in &lt;em&gt;C. plumiforme&lt;/em&gt;. (&lt;em&gt;A&lt;/em&gt;) The genomic synteny of the BGCs for momilactone formation in plants. (&lt;em&gt;B&lt;/em&gt;) Time course gene-expression profiles of the four genes (&lt;em&gt;CpDTC1&lt;/em&gt;/&lt;em&gt;HpDTC1&lt;/em&gt;, &lt;em&gt;CpMAS&lt;/em&gt;, &lt;em&gt;CpCYP&lt;/em&gt;&lt;em&gt;970A14&lt;/em&gt;, and &lt;em&gt;CpCYP&lt;/em&gt;&lt;em&gt;964A1&lt;/em&gt;) found in the momilactone BGC after the treatment of CuCl&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; or chitosan. &lt;em&gt;CpMAS&lt;/em&gt;: Unigene_5051, LC494432; &lt;em&gt;CpCYP&lt;/em&gt;&lt;em&gt;970A14&lt;/em&gt;: Unigene_16484, LC494433; &lt;em&gt;CpDTC1/HpDTC1&lt;/em&gt;: LC128408; &lt;em&gt;CpCYP&lt;/em&gt;&lt;em&gt;964A1&lt;/em&gt;: Unigene_12783, LC494434. The data shown were normalized by comparison to the expression of the housekeeping gene &lt;em&gt;CpACT3.&lt;/em&gt; (&lt;em&gt;C&lt;/em&gt;) Comparison of basal expression levels of the four genes in the cluster. The expression data represent the mean &#xB1; SD of three biological replicates with independent elicitor treatments to &lt;em&gt;C. plumiforme&lt;/em&gt; gametophytes.&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Fig. 2." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/117/22/12472/F2.medium.gif" width="440" height="420"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Fig. 2." src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/117/22/12472/F2.medium.gif" width="440" height="420"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/117/22/12472/F2.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Fig. 2." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li>
<li class="new-tab"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/117/22/12472/F2.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li>
<li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/929168" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li>
</ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Fig. 2.</span> <p id="p-21" class="first-child">The putative gene cluster for momilactone biosynthesis in <em>C. plumiforme</em>. (<em>A</em>) The genomic synteny of the BGCs for momilactone formation in plants. (<em>B</em>) Time course gene-expression profiles of the four genes (<em>CpDTC1</em>/<em>HpDTC1</em>, <em>CpMAS</em>, <em>CpCYP</em><em>970A14</em>, and <em>CpCYP</em><em>964A1</em>) found in the momilactone BGC after the treatment of CuCl<sub>2</sub> or chitosan. <em>CpMAS</em>: Unigene_5051, LC494432; <em>CpCYP</em><em>970A14</em>: Unigene_16484, LC494433; <em>CpDTC1/HpDTC1</em>: LC128408; <em>CpCYP</em><em>964A1</em>: Unigene_12783, LC494434. The data shown were normalized by comparison to the expression of the housekeeping gene <em>CpACT3.</em> (<em>C</em>) Comparison of basal expression levels of the four genes in the cluster. The expression data represent the mean ± SD of three biological replicates with independent elicitor treatments to <em>C. plumiforme</em> gametophytes.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-22">We previously reported that the biosynthesis of momilactones and relevant gene expression in <em>C. plumiforme</em> is induced by biotic and abiotic stresses, such as fungal infection and heavy metal treatment (<a id="xref-ref-9-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-9">9</a>). Therefore, we examined the expression profiles of the clustered genes in response to environmental stresses. To determine whether there was a coordinated transcription of the clustered momilactone biosynthesis genes in the <em>C. plumiforme</em> genome or not, RNA-seq data obtained from <em>C. plumiforme</em> CuCl<sub>2</sub>-treated foliage bodies were reanalyzed.</p><p id="p-23">Significant transcriptional induction was found in the four candidate genes clustered in the 150-kb region of Scaffold38 following CuCl<sub>2</sub>-induced stress in our RNA-seq analysis (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1914373117/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw" target="_blank"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S4</a>). We further verified the time course of gene expression for the four clustered genes (designated as <em>CpDTC1</em>/<em>HpDTC1</em>, <em>CpCYP970A14</em>, <em>CpCYP964A1</em>, and <em>CpMAS</em> based on their amino acid identities, described later) following both CuCl<sub>2</sub> and chitosan treatment using qRT-PCR. Treatment with both stress inducers resulted in significantly increased transcript levels of the four tandemly arranged genes by 6 to 12 h posttreatment (<a id="xref-fig-2-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2<em>B</em></a>). These results suggest high induction of the expression of all clustered genes in response to both abiotic and biotic stress, thereby inductively producing the bioactive momilactones for chemical defense. In addition, compared to their basal level of expression, the levels of <em>CpCYP</em><em>970A14</em>, <em>CpCYP</em><em>964A1</em>, and <em>CpMAS</em> genes, which would be responsible for the latter step of the pathway, were 30- to 100-fold higher than that of <em>CpDTC1</em>/<em>HpDTC1</em> under no treatment condition (<a id="xref-fig-2-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2<em>C</em></a>), suggesting that different types of transcriptional regulation are involved in the expression of each momilactone biosynthetic gene in the moss cluster.</p></div><div id="sec-5" class="subsection"><h3>Enzymatic Functions of the Clustered Genes for Momilactones.</h3><p id="p-24">To provide direct evidence that the clustered genes identified in the <em>Calohypnum</em> genome encode active enzymes involved in momilactone synthesis, we tested their enzymatic function. The predicted full-length 834 bp <em>CpMAS</em> cDNA was generated using RT-PCR. The cDNA encodes a 277-amino acid protein (LC494432) with 52.3% and 55.7% identity to rice OsMAS and barnyard grass EcMAS, respectively. Recombinant His-tagged CpMAS protein (His-CpMAS) was expressed in <em>Escherichia coli</em>, affinity-purified, and analyzed for enzymatic function in vitro by incubation with 3β-hydroxy-9β-pimara-7,15-dien-19,6β-olide (3OH-<em>syn</em>-pimaradienolide) and NAD<sup>+</sup> as substrates. As shown in <a id="xref-fig-3-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3<em>A</em></a>, the recombinant His-CpMAS protein catalyzed the formation of momilactone A [6] synthesis that could be easily identified following LC-MS/MS analysis (<a id="xref-fig-3-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3<em>B</em></a>). The identity of the momilactone A reaction product was further validated by GC-MS analysis (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1914373117/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw" target="_blank"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S5</a>). The result suggests that the CpMAS catalyzes the same enzymatic function as that done by the previously reported orthologous rice enzyme, OsMAS (<a id="xref-ref-8-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-8">8</a>).</p><div id="F3" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/117/22/12472/F3.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Functional analyses of clustered genes in Scaffold38.
