The relative amount of momilactone A to 3OH-pimaradienolide are shown. The data represent the mean &#xB1; SE of three biological replicates of independently agroinfiltrated &lt;em&gt;N. benthamiana&lt;/em&gt; leaves.&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Fig. 3." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/117/22/12472/F3.medium.gif" width="266" height="440"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Fig. 3." src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/117/22/12472/F3.medium.gif" width="266" height="440"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/117/22/12472/F3.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Fig. 3." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li>
<li class="new-tab"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/117/22/12472/F3.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li>
<li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/929518" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li>
</ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Fig. 3.</span> <p id="p-25" class="first-child">Functional analyses of clustered genes in Scaffold38. (<em>A</em>) The products and corresponding genes involved are indicated in the putative biosynthetic pathway of momilactones in <em>C. plumiforme</em>. Hypothetical intermediates are also shown in parentheses. (<em>B</em>, <em>Upper</em>) Momilactone A [6] converted from the substrate 3β-hydroxy-9βH-pimara-7,15-dien-19,6β-olide [5] by His-CpMAS protein is analyzed by LC-MS/MS. (<em>Top</em>) His-CpMAS products; (<em>Middle</em>) negative control without enzyme; (<em>Bottom</em>) authentic standards. (<em>B</em>, <em>Lower</em>) Product ion spectra of authentic momilactone A [6] and His-CpMAS product. (<em>C</em>) Mass chromatogram (scanned on <em>m/z</em> 316) of the reaction product (methyl ester derivative) obtained using GC-MS after incubation of <em>syn</em>-pimaradiene [2] with CpCYP970A14 (unigene16484), and OsCYP99A3. The mass spectra of the peaks on GC at 7.65 min are shown (<em>Right</em>). (<em>D</em>) Mass chromatogram (scanned on <em>m/z</em> 288) of the reaction product obtained using GC-MS after incubation of <em>syn</em>-pimaradiene [2] with CpCYP964A1 (Unigene12783). The mass spectra of the peaks on GC are shown (<em>Right</em>). (<em>E</em>) GC-MS mass chromatogram (scanned on <em>m/z</em> 272) of 9β-pimara-7,15-diene [2] produced by <em>N. benthamiana in planta</em> reaction. The mass spectra of the peaks on GC at 7.65 min are shown (<em>Right</em>). (<em>F</em>) Mass chromatograms and spectra of the reaction products from <em>N. benthamiana</em> leaves coexpressed CpDTC1/HpDTC1, CpCYP970A14, and CpCYP964A1. (<em>G</em>) LC-MS/MS analysis of the reaction products from <em>N. benthamiana</em> leaves coexpressed CpDTC1/HpDTC1, CpCYP970A14, and CpCYP964A1. The product ion spectra of the peak2 and authentic 3OH-pimaradienolide [5] are shown (<em>Right</em>). (<em>H</em>) Enhanced production of momilactone A in the <em>N. benthamiana</em> leaves coexpressed CpDTC1/HpDTC1, CpCYP970A14, CpCYP964A1 with or without CpMAS. The relative amount of momilactone A to 3OH-pimaradienolide are shown. The data represent the mean ± SE of three biological replicates of independently agroinfiltrated <em>N. benthamiana</em> leaves.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-26">To analyze the two putative CYPs in the cluster, full-length cDNAs (Unigene_16484 and Unigene_12783) corresponding to <em>CpCYP</em><em>970A14</em> and <em>CpCYP</em><em>964A1</em>, respectively, were amplified by RT-PCR and the amplification products were cloned into a <em>Pichia pastoris</em> yeast expression vector. The names of two CYPs were formally named by David Nelson according to the cytochrome P450 nomenclature system (<a id="xref-ref-24-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-24">24</a>). The full-length cDNA of <em>CpCYP</em><em>970A14</em> and <em>CpCYP</em><em>964A1</em> were determined to be 1,527 bp encoding 508 amino acids (LC494433), and 1,404 bp encoding 467 amino acids (LC494434), respectively. CpCYP970A14 has 39.1% identity to rice CYP71A1 and 41.8% identity to <em>Arabidopsis thaliana</em> CYP75B1, while CpCYP964A1 has 39.5% identity to rice CYP707A7 and 43.8% identity to <em>Selaginella moellendorffii</em> CYP707C1. Liquid cultures of the transformed yeast cells independently expressing the <em>CpCYP</em><em>970A14</em> and <em>CpCYP</em><em>964A1</em> cDNAs were fed <em>syn-</em>pimara-7,15-diene [2] and the enzymatic conversion products were analyzed by GC-MS. <em>Pichia</em> cells expressing the <em>CpCYP</em><em>970A14</em> cDNA exhibited the ability of the rice to produce <em>syn</em>-pimara-7,15-dien-19-oic acid [3] based on the direct comparison to the spectrum of the <em>syn</em>-pimara-7,15-dien-19-oic acid (as methyl ester derivative) produced by rice <em>CYP99A3</em>, which has previously been shown as <em>syn</em>-pimara-7,15-diene oxidase (<a id="xref-ref-25-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-25">25</a>) (<a id="xref-fig-3-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3<em>C</em></a>). Negative control yeast harboring only vector plasmid showed no corresponding signal. This result indicated that CpCYP970A14 unequivocally catalyzes oxidation of the C19 methyl group of <em>syn</em>-pimara-7,15-diene [2] to form the momilactone biosynthetic precursor <em>syn</em>-pimara-7,15-dien-19-oic acid [3] (<a id="xref-fig-3-4" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3<em>A</em></a>). When the <em>Pichia</em> yeast cells expressing <em>CpCYP</em><em>964A1</em> were fed with <em>syn</em>-pimara-7,15-diene, a product was detected in the windows of <em>m/z</em> 288 selected ion scanning, whereas the negative control did not show any significant peak. The mass spectrum of the product was identical to that of authentic 3β-hydroxy-9β-pimara-7,15-diene [4] reported previously, suggesting that CpCYP964A1 is responsible for catalyzing hydroxylation at the C3 position of <em>syn</em>-pimara-7,15-diene [2] (<a id="xref-fig-3-5" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3<em>D</em></a>) (<a id="xref-ref-26-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-26">26</a>, <a id="xref-ref-27-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-27">27</a>).</p><p id="p-27">To provide further validation of the <em>C. plumiforme</em> momilactone A biosynthetic gene function, we also examined their function by transiently expressing them in <em>Nicotiana benthamiana</em>. The <em>CpDTC1</em>/<em>HpDTC1</em>, <em>CpCYP</em><em>970A14</em>, and <em>CpCYP</em><em>964A1</em> genes were cloned into a pEAQ-HT expression vector (<a id="xref-ref-28-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-28">28</a>) and then introduced into <em>N. benthamiana</em> leaves using the Agroinfiltration method. As shown in <a id="xref-fig-3-6" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3<em>E</em></a>, it was possible to detect the <em>in planta</em> production of <em>syn</em>-pimaradiene following transient expression of <em>CpDTC1</em>/<em>HpDTC1</em> [2]. When <em>CpCYP</em><em>970A14</em> was coexpressed with <em>CpDTC1</em>/<em>HpDTC1</em> in <em>N. benthamiana</em>, it was possible to detect the <em>in planta</em> conversion of <em>syn</em>-pimaradiene [2] to <em>syn</em>-pimara-7,15-dien-19-oic acid [3] (<a id="xref-fig-3-7" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3<em>F</em></a>). This was based on direct comparison to the spectrum of the <em>syn</em>-pimara-7,15-dien-19-oic acid (as methyl ester derivative) successfully produced by rice <em>CYP99A3</em> in <em>N. benthamiana</em> (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1914373117/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw" target="_blank"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S6</a>). Similarly, <em>CpCYP</em><em>964A1</em> was also found to catalyze the conversion from <em>syn</em>-pimaradiene [2] to 3β-hydroxy-9β-pimara-7,15-diene [4] in <em>N. benthamiana</em> (<a id="xref-fig-3-8" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3<em>F</em></a>). These enzymatic activities were identical to what was observed in our yeast expression system (<a id="xref-fig-3-9" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3<em>D</em></a>).</p><p id="p-28">When both CpCYP970A14 and CpCYP964A1 were coexpressed, neither <em>syn</em>-pimaradienoic acid [3] nor 3β-hydroxy-9β-pimara-7,15-diene [4] produced by CpCYP970A14 or CpCYP964A1 could be detected (<a id="xref-fig-3-10" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3<em>F</em></a>), presumably due to a further metabolism into a hypothetical intermediate 3OH-<em>syn</em>-pimaradienoic acid (<a id="xref-fig-3-11" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3<em>A</em></a>, compound X). However, there was no relevant signal corresponding to the expected molecular ion (<em>m/z</em> 332 as methyl ester derivative) of compound X in GC-MS analysis (<a id="xref-fig-3-12" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3<em>F</em></a>). On the other hand, synthesis of 3OH-<em>syn</em>-pimaradienolide [5] was detected using LC-MS/MS (<a id="xref-fig-3-13" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3<em>G</em></a>, peak 2). Furthermore, accumulation of momilactone A [6] was more clearly defined in the <em>N. benthamiana</em> leaves expressing <em>CpDTC1/HpDTC1</em>, <em>CpCYP</em><em>970A14</em>, and <em>CpCYP</em><em>964A1</em> (<a id="xref-fig-3-14" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3<em>G</em></a>, peak 1). These results imply that CpCYP970A14 and <em>CpCYP964A1</em> would possibly be able to catalyze two oxidation steps in conversion from <em>syn</em>-pimaradienoic acid [3] and 3β-hydroxy-9β-pimara-7,15-diene [4] to 3OH-<em>syn</em>-pimaradienolide [5] via the hypothetical intermediate 3,6OH-<em>syn</em>-pimaradienoic acid (<a id="xref-fig-3-15" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3<em>A</em></a>, compound Y). We also found that the potential enzymatic activity of momilactone A [6] synthesis from 3OH-<em>syn</em>-pimaradienolide [5] existed endogenously in <em>N. benthamiana</em> leaves, based on in vitro assays with crude protein and <em>in planta</em> feeding assays (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1914373117/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw" target="_blank"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S7</a>). Eventually, coexpression of <em>CpMAS</em> with other clustered genes increased the production of momilactone A (<a id="xref-fig-3-16" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3<em>H</em></a>), and <em>in planta</em> conversion of 3OH-<em>syn</em>-pimaradienolide to momilactone A was apparently enhanced by <em>CpMAS</em> expression in <em>N. benthamiana</em> as compared to that in the vector control plant (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1914373117/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw" target="_blank"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S8</a>).</p><p id="p-29">Taken together, these results indicate that the two <em>CYP</em> genes adjacent to the <em>syn</em>-pimara-7,15-diene synthase gene <em>CpDTC1</em>/<em>HpDTC1</em> and the momilactone A synthase gene <em>CpMAS</em> are functionally involved in momilactone A formation, supporting our conclusion that this is in fact a BGC for momilactone biosynthesis in the moss. These results are also a demonstration of the successful reconstitution of the momilactone biosynthetic pathway by heterologous gene expression in momilactone nonproducing plants.</p></div><div id="sec-6" class="subsection"><h3>Evolution of Momilactone Gene Clusters in Plants.</h3><p id="p-30">To investigate the evolutionary trajectory of the momilactone BGCs, we broadly surveyed existence of any other possible momilactone gene clusters in the plant kingdom. To this end, we collected available public genomic sequence and annotation information of 107 plants and conducted a data-mining exercise on this genomic information. We first classified all genes into four types based on the Pfam domain annotation of momilactone genes in <em>O. sativa</em>, namely CYP genes (Pfam ID PF00067), terpene synthase (TPS) genes (Pfam ID PF01397/PF03936), short-chain dehydrogenase/reductase (SDR), MAS genes (Pfam ID PF13561/PF00106/PF08659), and all others (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1914373117/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw" target="_blank"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S9</a>). Next, we scanned all 107 plant genomes within a window of 100 kb using in-house scripts to select candidate regions where <em>TPS</em>, <em>P450</em>, and <em>SDR</em> genes coexisted, and the adjacent windows holding these three types of genes would be merged. As a result, in addition to the known momilactone gene clusters in <em>Oryza</em> species and barnyard grass (<a id="xref-ref-8-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-8">8</a>, <a id="xref-ref-16-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-16">16</a>), 43 putative TPS gene clusters in 35 plant species were identified in seed plants, whereas no such candidate gene clusters were found in bryophytes other than in <em>C. plumiforme</em>, fern, and gymnosperm (<a id="xref-fig-4-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Fig. 4</a> and <a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1914373117/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw" target="_blank"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S6</a>). No apparent syntenic relationship in gene arrangement was observed between the known momilactone gene cluster and the 43 candidate clusters.</p><div id="F4" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/117/22/12472/F4.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Genome-wide identification of the clustered genes for momilactone biosynthesis in plants. Black boxes represent existence of the genes in the corresponding plant genome. Types of gene clustering: CL1, DTC, or DTC-like type of terpene synthase gene + P450 + MAS; CL2, one type of terpene synthase gene (CPS or KS or DTC or DTC-like) + P450 + MAS; CL3, two types of terpene synthase genes (CPS and KSL) + P450 + MAS. Momilactones were only detected in the species with CL1 or CL3 gene clusters." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-193330918" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;Genome-wide identification of the clustered genes for momilactone biosynthesis in plants. Black boxes represent existence of the genes in the corresponding plant genome. Types of gene clustering: CL1, DTC, or DTC-like type of terpene synthase gene + P450 + MAS; CL2, one type of terpene synthase gene (CPS or KS or DTC or DTC-like) + P450 + MAS; CL3, two types of terpene synthase genes (CPS and KSL) + P450 + MAS. Momilactones were only detected in the species with CL1 or CL3 gene clusters.&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Fig. 4." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/117/22/12472/F4.medium.gif" width="419" height="440"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Fig. 4." src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/117/22/12472/F4.medium.gif" width="419" height="440"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/117/22/12472/F4.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Fig. 4." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li>
<li class="new-tab"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/117/22/12472/F4.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li>
<li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/929385" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li>
</ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Fig. 4.</span> <p id="p-31" class="first-child">Genome-wide identification of the clustered genes for momilactone biosynthesis in plants. Black boxes represent existence of the genes in the corresponding plant genome. Types of gene clustering: CL1, DTC, or DTC-like type of terpene synthase gene + P450 + MAS; CL2, one type of terpene synthase gene (CPS or KS or DTC or DTC-like) + P450 + MAS; CL3, two types of terpene synthase genes (CPS and KSL) + P450 + MAS. Momilactones were only detected in the species with CL1 or CL3 gene clusters.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-32">Homologs of the bifunctional DTC gene <em>CpDTC1</em>/<em>HpDTC1</em>, a key pathway gene responsible for producing the core diterpene substrate, could be identified in other bryophytes, such as <em>P. patens</em> and fern species, but were not found in flowering plants (<a id="xref-table-wrap-2-1" class="xref-table" href="#T2">Table 2</a>). We identified many terpene synthases in flowering plants with the two D-rich motifs (i.e., “DXDD” and “DDXXD” motif); however, we termed them as “DTC-like” because their DXDD motif was not located at its typical position (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1914373117/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw" target="_blank"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S10<em>A</em></a>) and they lacked the third motif (“SXYDTAW”). Interestingly, we could not find any terpene cyclases in the algal species examined. Phylogenetic analysis of the terpene biosynthesis-related genes in the momilactone gene clusters of the two grasses indicated that the <em>CPS</em> and <em>KSL</em> genes in the BGCs did not appear to have evolved from the clustered terpene cyclase gene (<em>CpDTC1</em>/<em>HpDTC1</em>) in the moss (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1914373117/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw" target="_blank"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Figs. S10<em>B</em> and S11</a>).</p><div id="T2" class="table pos-float"><div class="table-inline table-callout-links"><div class="callout"><span>View this table:</span><ul class="callout-links"><li class="view-inline first"><a href="##" class="table-expand-inline" data-table-url="/highwire/markup/929298/expansion?postprocessors=highwire_tables%2Chighwire_reclass%2Chighwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_inline_linked_media%2Chighwire_embed&amp;table-expand-inline=1" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View inline</a></li>
<li class="view-popup last"><a href="/highwire/markup/929298/expansion?width=1000&amp;height=500&amp;iframe=true&amp;postprocessors=highwire_tables%2Chighwire_reclass%2Chighwire_figures%2Chighwire_math%2Chighwire_inline_linked_media%2Chighwire_embed" class="colorbox colorbox-load table-expand-popup" rel="gallery-fragment-tables" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">View popup</a></li>
</ul></div></div><div class="table-caption"><span class="table-label">Table 2.</span> <p id="p-33" class="first-child">Summary of the TPS family in plant genomes</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-35">Up to now, only two types of gene clusters (CL1 and CL3) have been shown to contain genes involved in the biosynthesis of momilactones (<a id="xref-fig-4-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Fig. 4</a>). Intriguingly, we found that <em>A. thaliana</em> also has a potential CL1-type gene cluster that contains a DTC-like gene and CYP and MAS gene homologs. However, the DTC-like gene has been reported to be <em>TPS10</em> [coding for (3<em>R</em>)-linalool synthase] and it is coexpressed with the CYP genes, <em>CYP71B31</em> and <em>CYP76C3</em>, which are located outside the cluster region that further metabolizes (3<em>R</em>)-linalool into hydroxylinalool (<a id="xref-ref-29-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-29">29</a>). Therefore, the only gene cluster harboring a DTC-type bifunctional terpene cyclase gene for momilactone formation found in our survey is that present in <em>C. plumiforme</em>.</p><p id="p-36">While our results suggest that the 43 candidate clusters are not likely to be responsible for the production of momilactones, they are potentially involved in the formation of other terpenoid metabolites. The lack of synteny and phylogenetic distance supports the conclusion that the momilactone BGCs in <em>O. sativa</em>, <em>E. crus-galli</em>, and <em>C. plumiforme</em> arose independently with acquisition of conserved core component enzymes of <em>syn</em>-pimaradiene synthase, CYPs, and SDR. Notably, the momilactone BGC in <em>C. plumiforme</em> seems to be a unique case of clustering of biosynthetic genes responsible for a specialized secondary metabolite for plant defense and allelopathy in nonseed plants.</p></div></div><div class="section discussion" id="sec-7"><h2 class="">Discussion</h2><p id="p-37" class="flushleft">In this study, we have generated a high-quality genome assembly of the moss, <em>C. plumiforme</em>, and shown that it contains a BGC responsible for the production of the defense and allelopathic metabolite momilactone. This is the only evidence of gene clustering for the biosynthesis of specialized defensive metabolites identified in a bryophyte lineage and is distinct from those previously identified in the grasses, which includes <em>O. sativa</em>, wild <em>Oryza</em> species, and the paddy weed barnyard grass <em>E. crus-galli</em>. This observation suggests that the evolutionary process of BGC formation in all plants is widely distributed. Our evidence indicates that there is little syntenic relationship among the momilactone BGCs in <em>C. plumiforme</em> and the grasses. Therefore, momilactone biosynthesis gene clustering in the plant genome was likely the product of convergent evolution leading to the formation of the same distinctive secondary metabolite (momilactone) in plants from very different families (mosses and grasses). How and why this occurred is unknown, but it is likely tied to the necessity of maintaining a capability to regulate the formation of this important defense and allelopathic molecule.</p><p id="p-38">The four genes in the <em>C. plumiforme</em> momilactone BGC (i.e., <em>CpMAS</em>, <em>CpCYP</em><em>970A14</em>, <em>CpDTC1</em>/<em>HpDTC1</em>, and <em>CpCYP</em><em>964A1</em>) are coordinately induced under stress treatment (<a id="xref-fig-2-4" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2<em>B</em></a>). Among the four genes, <em>CpDTC1</em>/<em>HpDTC1</em> had a very low basal level of expression (based on its fragments per kilobase of transcript per million mapped reads value of 9.23 in untreated samples in the RNA-seq analysis) and consequently the highest level of induction (85-fold with chitosan treatment and 540-fold with CuCl<sub>2</sub> treatment as determined by qRT-PCR). In contrast, the other three genes in the cluster had higher basal expression levels than <em>CpDTC1</em>/<em>HpDTC1</em> and were more up-regulated with the chitosan (by 4- to 24-fold) and CuCl<sub>2</sub> (by 8- to 150-fold) treatments than that in untreated samples. Since <em>C. plumiforme</em> constitutively produces momilactones under natural conditions (<a id="xref-ref-9-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-9">9</a>), the induction of <em>CpDTC1</em>/<em>HpDTC1</em> expression seems to be the major regulatory step controlling momilactone production in response to stress. The low-level induction of the <em>CpMAS</em>, <em>CpCYP</em><em>970A14</em>, and <em>CpCYP</em><em>964A1</em> genes involved in momilactone biosynthesis in moss is in contrast to that observed in the orthologous genes of the momilactone BGC in <em>O. sativa</em>. Here it has been observed that all of the rice genes in the cluster are highly induced in response to environmental stress (<a id="xref-ref-30-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-30">30</a>). Clearly, additional studies are needed to determine the regulatory elements and transcription factors important in determining the differential levels of gene expression of the momilactone biosynthetic genes in the <em>C. plumiforme</em> cluster. Studies toward understanding the control mechanisms of the gene regulatory process, compared to those involved in regulation of the genes in the momilactone BGCs in various grasses, are also warranted. The availability of the <em>C. plumiforme</em> genome sequence and gene annotation data will certainly assist in these future studies.</p><p id="p-39">Several BGC prediction pipelines have been developed for the plant genomes, including plantiSMASH (<a id="xref-ref-31-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-31">31</a>), PlantClusterFinder (<a id="xref-ref-32-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-32">32</a>), and PhytoClust (<a id="xref-ref-33-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-33">33</a>). However, there are still limitations in the use of prediction methods with bryophyte genomes, which have a long divergence distance to flowering plants. In our genome survey, we added domain searching in our analysis pipeline for P450 and ADH domains. It is also known that biochemical and chemical analyses are indispensable to fully delineate newly predicted pathways associated with candidate BGCs (<a id="xref-ref-34-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-34">34</a>). Hence, evidence from gene expression and functional experiments were additionally used to support the prediction of the momilactone BGC in <em>C. plumiforme</em>. As a result, we found that four genes existed as a cluster in one contig (Scaffold38) in our BGC prediction. This potential gene cluster is composed of <em>CpDTC1/HpDTC1</em> (<em>syn</em>-pimaradiene synthase), two <em>P450</em> genes (<em>CpCYP</em><em>970A14</em> and <em>CpCYP</em><em>964A1</em>, and a dehydrogenase gene (<em>CpMAS</em>). CpMAS is highly similar to OsMAS, a rice momilactone A synthase at the amino acid level, and our functional analysis of <em>E. coli</em>-produced recombinant CpMAS protein showed the activity for momilactone A synthase. On the other hand, although the two P450s in the cluster, CpCYP970A14 and CpCYP964A1, do not share high levels of identity to the two known P450s, CYP99A2 and CYP99A3, involved in the momilactone A biosynthesis in rice, our functional verification of CpCYP970A14 and CpCYP964A1 using two different approaches (i.e., expression in yeast and transient expression in tobacco) demonstrated their direct involvement in momilactone A biosynthesis. Moreover, the successful reconstitution of the momilactone biosynthetic pathway in <em>N. benthamina</em> suggests that only two P450s (CpCYP970A14 and CpCYP964A1) are required for the formation of the momilactone A precursor 3OH-<em>syn</em>-pimaradienolide [5] in the moss. This is in contrast with rice, which appears to require at least three P450s (CYP99A2/A3, CYP707A8, and CYP76M8) in the oxidation step. The <em>CpCYP</em><em>964A1</em> gene is responsible for the conversion of <em>syn</em>-pimaradiene to 3OH-<em>syn</em>-pimaradiene and the <em>CpCYP</em><em>970A14</em> gene is involved in the synthesis of <em>syn</em>-pimaradienoic acid. Our results suggest that either, or both, are capable of catalyzing two consecutive oxidation steps at the C19 and C6 positions to yield 3OH-<em>syn</em>-pimaradienolide [5] through a spontaneous lactonization of a putative intermediate 3β, 6β-dihydroxy-9β-pimara-7,15-dien-19-oic acid. However, we currently do not know if such a putative intermediate is produced by the consecutive reactions catalyzed by <em>CpCYP</em><em>970A14</em> and <em>CpCYP</em><em>964A1</em>. Further studies will clearly be necessary to establish the whole pathway of the momilactone A and B synthesis and its precise biosynthetic machinery in the moss.</p><p id="p-40">A distribution of TPS and potential gene clusters in distinctive plant families were evidenced by our genomic survey in the plant kingdom (<a id="xref-fig-4-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Fig. 4</a>). It appears that many vascular plants have CL2-type gene clusters comprising one type of terpene cyclase gene (CPS or KSL) with CYP and MAS homologs. However, these clusters are not likely to be responsible for the momilactone synthesis, and further investigation will be required to understand if they are functional metabolic gene clusters for specialized metabolite production in each plant. Based on previous studies of terpenoid biosynthesis in bryophytes, DTC in mosses are shown to be bifunctional enzymes possessing both CPS (class I/type B, DXDD motif only) and KSL (class II/type A, DDXXD motif only) activities (<a id="xref-ref-9-5" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-9">9</a>). This is distinct from the situation in vascular plants where separate CPS- and KSL-type genes are present in the genome (<a id="xref-ref-8-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-8">8</a>). Intriguingly, three bryophytes also appear to possess potential homologs of CPS and KSL, and DTC-like gene homologs are present in most vascular plants surveyed in this study (<a id="xref-fig-4-4" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Fig. 4</a>). However, the exact types of terpenoids produced by these TPSs remain to be determined.</p><p id="p-41">Our phylogenetic analysis of TPS genes in 107 plant genomes also clearly shows that only nonseed plants have the bifunctional TPS gene, such as <em>CpDTC1</em>/<em>HpDTC1</em>, including four bryophytes (<em>M. polymorpha</em>, <em>C. plumiforme</em>, <em>P. patens</em>, and <em>Sphagnum fallax</em>) and two ferns (<em>S. moellendorffii</em> and <em>Azolla filiculoides</em>) (<a id="xref-table-wrap-2-2" class="xref-table" href="#T2">Table 2</a>). Among these lower plants harboring bifunctional TPS, only <em>C. plumiforme</em> has the ability to produce momilactone (<a id="xref-fig-4-5" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Fig. 4</a>). We found five homologs of <em>CpDTC1</em>/<em>HpDTC1</em> in the <em>C. plumiforme</em> genome, whereas <em>P. patens</em> only had one copy of the homologous gene, annotated as <em>ent</em>-kaurene synthase (KS) involved in gibberellin-like ancient plant hormone synthesis (<a id="xref-table-wrap-2-3" class="xref-table" href="#T2">Table 2</a> and <a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1914373117/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw" target="_blank"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S7</a>) (<a id="xref-ref-35-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-35">35</a>). This observation supports our hypothesis that gene duplication and neofunctionalization likely occurred in <em>C. plumiforme</em>, leading to the evolution of <em>KS</em> variants with the ability to synthesize <em>syn</em>-pimaradiene, a distinct precursor of momilactone. Additionally, our phylogenetic analysis clearly shows that <em>CpDTC1</em>/<em>HpDTC1</em> is derived from a bifunctional CPS/KS but not from monofunctional KS (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1914373117/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw" target="_blank"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Figs. S10<em>B</em> and S11</a>). The CYP genes in the momilactone BGCs in moss and grasses were found to have different phylogenetic affiliations, with the two CYPs in <em>C. plumiforme</em> (<em>CpCYP</em><em>970A14</em> and <em>CpCYP</em><em>964A1</em>) belonging to the CYP71A (85 clan) and CYP707A (71 clan) subfamilies on one branch, whereas the CYPs in the momilactone BGC in <em>O. sativa</em> and <em>E. crus-galli</em> (<em>CYP99A</em> and <em>CYP76M</em>, respectively) are located on a different branch (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1914373117/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw" target="_blank"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S12</a>). These data further support the hypothesis that the molecular evolution of the relevant CYPs responsible for the momilactone biosynthesis occurred independently in these plant species. The origin of the final step of momilactone synthesis catalyzed by MAS is less clear. It appears that many SDR have a potential dehydrogenation activity capable of converting 3OH-<em>syn</em>-pimaradienolide to produce momilactone A, likely because of their low substrate specificity. In fact, <em>N. benthamiana</em> and fission yeast have been found to have the endogenous metabolic capacity to catalyze the conversion of 3OH-<em>syn</em>-pimaradienolide to momilactone A (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1914373117/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw" target="_blank"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Figs. S7 and S13</a>).</p><p id="p-42">Functional gene clustering and coordinated expression leading to the production of a specialized metabolite clearly provides a distinctive advantage in response to environmental perturbations, such as abiotic and biotic stress and for dealing with competition with other organisms for space and resources in an uncertain ecology. The observation that bryophytes like <em>C. plumiforme</em> independently evolved a momilactone BGC, such as that found in grasses, underscores the value of this molecule as an important, broad-spectrum agent useful in defense and allelopathy, and indicates the power of gene duplication and neofunctionalization in plant evolution. The defense and allelopathic function could have been part of the positive selection pressure for assembling the BGC in <em>C. plumiforme</em>, as previously suggested (<a id="xref-ref-36-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-36">36</a>). In addition, it has also been suggested that negative selection pressure could play a role in BGC assembly if inheritance of single genes or incomplete BGCs leads to the build-up of detrimental pathway intermediates (<a id="xref-ref-4-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-4">4</a>, <a id="xref-ref-36-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-36">36</a>). It is currently not known whether any intermediates in the momilactone biosynthetic pathway have deleterious effects on plant metabolism or growth. This is certainly an area that could be tested in the future using gene-editing technologies and would contribute to the understanding of the contributions of positive and negative selective pressures in momilactone BGC evolution.</p><p id="p-43">The convergent evolution of distinctive pathways leading to the formation of momilactones indicated the profligate nature of TPSs and their ability to provide new combinations of actions to extend plant biochemistry and the production of need-driven specialized plant metabolites. These results set the stage for future work aimed at examining a broader group of bryophytes to determine whether they produce momilactones and have similar gene clusters, as well as determining the extent to which gene clusters for specialized metabolites occur broadly in ancient plants.</p></div><div class="section materials-methods" id="sec-8"><h2 class="">Materials and Methods</h2><div id="sec-9" class="subsection"><h3>Plant Material and Genome Sequencing.</h3><p id="p-44">The previously reported wild-type strain of <em>C. plumiform</em>e was cultured on BCDATG agar medium (<a id="xref-ref-37-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-37">37</a>) under continuous white light at 24 °C and was used throughout this study. Genomic DNA was extracted from the <em>C. plumiforme</em> gametophore using DNeasy Plant Mini Kit (Qiagen). The <em>C. plumiforme</em> genome was sequenced using both the Illumina HiSEq. 4000 and PacBio RS II sequencing platforms. In total, 88 Gb of Illumina HiSEq. 4000 sequencing data (203× genome coverage) was generated, including two paired-end libraries with insertion sizes of 360 bp and 350 bp and two mate-pair libraries with insertion sizes of 10 kb and 20 kb (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1914373117/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw" target="_blank"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S1</a>). A total of 1.54 Gb of Pacbio sequencing data (3.5× genome coverage) with an average subreads length of 5.26 kb was generated with the PacBio RS II platform, including one SMRT cell from P5C3 chemistry.</p></div><div id="sec-10" class="subsection"><h3>De Novo Genome Assembly, Genome Annotation, and Identification of Gene Clusters.</h3><p id="p-45">All Illumina and Pacbio data were used to perform hybrid assembly and genome annotation and gene-clustering identification were conducted with methods described in <a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1914373117/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw" target="_blank"><em>SI Appendix</em>, <em>SI Materials and Methods</em></a>.</p></div><div id="sec-11" class="subsection"><h3>Phylogenetic Tree and Divergence Time Estimation.</h3><p id="p-46">Based on the annotated protein sequences from <em>C. plumiforme</em> and the other 10 plants (<em>P. patens</em>, <em>M. polymorpha</em>, <em>Picea abies</em>, <em>Ginkgo biloba</em>, <em>Amborella trichopoda</em>, <em>A. thaliana</em>, <em>O. sativa</em>, <em>O. punctata</em>, <em>E. crus-galli</em>, and <em>Chara vulgaris</em>); orthogroups or gene families were identified using Orthofinder with default setting (<a id="xref-ref-38-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-38">38</a>). After the Gblocks alignment optimization (<a id="xref-ref-39-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-39">39</a>), the alignment of single-copy genes by MAFFT (<a id="xref-ref-40-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-40">40</a>) was used to estimate species divergence times by an uncorrelated relaxed clock in BEAST v1.10.4, with <em>C. vulgaris</em> as an outgroup (<a id="xref-ref-41-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-41">41</a>). See <a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1914373117/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw" target="_blank"><em>SI Appendix</em>, <em>SI Materials and Methods</em></a> for the details.</p></div><div id="sec-12" class="subsection"><h3>Functional Analysis of Clustered Genes.</h3><p id="p-47">For functional analysis of clustered genes, see <a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1914373117/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw" target="_blank"><em>SI Appendix</em>, <em>SI Materials and Methods</em></a>.</p></div><div id="sec-13" class="subsection"><h3>Data Availability.</h3><p id="p-48">The genome assembly and full-length cDNAs of <em>C. plumiforme</em> by this study have been deposited in the Genome Sequence Archive (GSA) under accession no. PRJCA001833 and DNA Data Bank of Japan under accession nos. LC494432 (<em>CpMAS</em>), LC494433 (<em>CpCYP</em><em>970A14</em>), and LC494434 (<em>CpCYP</em><em>964A1</em>), respectively.</p></div></div><div class="section ack" id="ack-1"><h2>Acknowledgments</h2><p id="p-49">We thank Prof. Toshiyuki Ohnishi (Shizuoka University) for providing the plasmid pRI-ATR1, and Prof. George Lomonossoff (John Innes Centre) for <em>N. benthamiana</em> expression vector pEAQ-HT. We also thank Professor David R. Nelson (University of Tennessee, Memphis, TN) for giving the gene names of <em>C. plumiforme</em> P450s. This work was supported by grants from the National Natural Science Foundation (9143511), Zhejiang Natural Science Foundation (LZ17C130001), Jiangsu Collaborative Innovation Center for Modern Crop Production and 111 Project B17039 (to L.F.), and Grants-in-Aid for Scientific Research - KAKENHI 17H03811 (to K.O.) and 19H02894 (to H. Kawaide).</p></div><div class="section fn-group" id="fn-group-1"><h2>Footnotes</h2><ul class="pnas-footnotes"><li class="fn-equal" id="fn-3"><p id="p-3"><a class="rev-xref" href="#xref-fn-3-1">↵</a><sup>1</sup>L.M., H.
